Whitney anukramaс╣З─л

[Brahman.тАФaс╣гс╣н─Бviс╣Е┼Ыakam. ─Бrsy (─Бrtvy?) ─Бyuс╣гyam. tr─Бiс╣гс╣нubham*: 1, 2, 7. bhurij; 3. ─Бst─Бrapan╠Дkti; 4. prast─Бrapan╠Дkti; 6. pathy─Бpan╠Дkti; 8. purast─Бj jyotiс╣гmat─л jagat─л; 9. 5-p. jagat─л; 11. viс╣гс╣н─Бrapan╠Дkti; 12. purast─Бdbс╣Ыhat─л; 14. 3-av. 6-p. jagat─л; 15. pathy─Бpan╠Дkti; 19. upariс╣гс╣н─Бdbс╣Ыhat─л; 21. sataс╕еpan╠Дkti; 26. ─Бst─Бrapan╠Дkti; 22, 28. purast─Бdbс╣Ыhat─л; 5, 10, 16-18, 20, 23-25, 27. anuс╣гс╣нubh (17. trip─Бt).]

Whitney

Comment

Found also in P─Бipp. xvi., all but the last verse, and with 9 before 8. *тМКVerse 13 appears to be the one upon the strength of which the Anukr. declares the hymn to be tr─Бiс╣гс╣нubham (its remaining 27 vss. being exceptions!); and even this is no real triс╣гс╣нubh. It counts indeed 44 (8 + 12: 12 + 12) and might be called purast─Бj jyotiс╣гmat─л.тМЛ

тМКV─Бit. uses only vs. 16: see under 16.тАФThe uses by K─Бu┼Ы. are many. For the uses of this hymn with h. 1, see introd. to h. 1. Further, in the name-giving ceremony, it is used (58. 14) with pouring a continuous stream of water on the youth’s right hand; and this is followed (58. 15) by the binding on of an amulet of deodar (see note to vs. 28 below); and the use of vs. 16 is especially prescribed (58. 17: the text of the s─лtra in the comm. differs from that of Bl.) to accompany the putting a new garment upon him. Vss. 12-13 are prescribed (97. 3) in case of family quarrels (see also note to vs. 9 below); vs. 14 (comm., 14-15) is used in the tonsure ceremony (54.17); and again vs. 14 (comm., 14-15), on the child’s first going out of the house (58. 18). Vs. 17 was previously prescribed for the same tonsure ceremony (53. 19: the comm. reads kс╣гuram abhyukс╣гya triс╕е pram─Бrс╣гс╣нi) on sprinkling and wiping the razor; and the same vs. is substituted for vi. 68. 3 by the Da┼Ыa Karm─Бс╣Зi in the same ceremony (53. 17 note); furthermore, it is used at the beginning of the ceremony of the reception of the Vedic student (55. 3). Vs. 18 (comm., 18-19) is used on the first feeding of the child (with rice and barley: 58. 19); and vss. 20 and 22 on his “committal” (58. 20, 21) respectively “to day and night” and “to the seasons."тАФBloomfield (note to 58. 17) cites a passage describing the four “committals”: 1. to heaven and earth, with vss. 14-15; 2. to rice and barley, with vss. 18-19; 3. to day and night, with vs. 20; 4. to the seasons, with vs. 22.тАФFinally, the comm. regards vs. 15 as intended, with v. 1. 7 etc., at K─Бu┼Ы. 46. 1-3, in the rite against false accusation.тМЛ

Translations

Translated: Muir, v. 447; Ludwig, p. 496; Henry, 4, 39; Griffith, i. 388; Bloomfield, 55, 573.

Griffith

режрез рдЖ рд░рднрд╕реНрд╡реЗрдорд╛рдордореГрддрд╕реНрдп ...{Loading}...
Whitney
Translation
  1. Take thou hold. on this bundle (?) of immortality; unsevered length
    of life be thine; I bring back thy life, [thy] life-time; go not to
    the welkin (r├бjas), to darkness; do not perish.
Notes

SPP. with all his authorities save one (which has sn├║-) reads
┼Ыn├║с╣гс╣нim in a, and this must doubtless be regarded as the true AV.
text: compare iii. 17. 2. The comm. glosses it here with prasnuti ‘a
dripping forth,’ and then explains amс╣Ыtasya ┼Ыnuс╣гс╣нi as the stream of
water which, according to one direction in K─Бu┼Ы. (58. 14), is to be
poured out while the hymn is recited. He glosses rajas with r─Бga,
and explains it and tamas as the two familiar guс╣Зas so called: it
is, indeed, a little startling to find the two names here side by side.

Griffith

Seize to thyself this trust of life for ever: thine be longevity which nothing shortens. Thy spirit and thy life again I bring thee: die not, nor vanish into mist and darkness.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Come thou hitherward unto the light of the living; I take thee in
    order to life for a hundred autumns; loosening down the fetters of
    death, imprecation, I set for thee further a longer life-time.
Notes

Some of SPP’s mss. accent falsely abhy├иhi in a. Ppp. transposes
the order of c and d, and reads lokam for arv─Бn╠Д in a.

Griffith

Come to the light of living men, come hither: I draw thee to a life of hundred autumns. Loosing the bonds of Death, the curse that holds thee, I give thee age of very long duration.

Whitney
Translation
  1. From the wind have I found thy breath, from the sun I thy sight; what
    is thy mind, that I maintain in thee; be in concord with thy limbs;
    speak with thy tongue, not babbling.
Notes

The comm. reads in d vi┼Ыv─Бn╠Дg─Бis and ─Бlapan.

Griffith

Thy breath have I recovered from the Wind, thy vision from the Sun. Thy mind I stablish and secure within thee: feel in thy members,. use thy tongue, conversing.

Whitney
Translation
  1. I blow together upon thee with the breath of bipeds [and]
    quadrupeds as upon [new-] born fire; homage, O death, to thy sight,
    homage to thy breath have I made.
Notes
Griffith

I blow upon thee with the breath of bipeds and quadrupeds, as on a fire new-kindled. To thee, O Death, and to thy sight and breath have I paid reverence.

Let this man live, let him not die: we raise him, we recover him. I make for him a healing balm. O Death, forbear to slay this man.

Whitney
Translation
  1. The lively, by-no-means-harming, living herb, the preserving,
    overpowering, powerful, do I call hither, for this man’s freedom from
    harm.
Notes

тМКP─Бdas a and b are repeated at viii. 7. 6.тМЛ The accent of the
two participles j─лvant─л╠Бm and tr─Бyam─Бс╣З─Б╠Бm seems to mark them as

nagham─Бr├б, seems a fusion of the phrase na gh─Б (or gha) riс╣гyati
etc.; the pada-mss. chance mostly to agree in the frequent error of
reading с╣Ы for ri (naghaре░с╣Ыс╣г─Б╠Бm; Bp. ре░riс╣г─Б╠Бm); the comm. reads and
explains nagharuс╣г─Бm, taking -gha- as representing root han:
yasy─Бс╕е kopo тАШpi na gh─Бtakaс╕е; he regards the plant intended as the
paths (Clypea hernandifolia). Ppp. reads nagh─Бriс╣гaс╣Б, adds
arundhat─лm after sahasvat─лm in d, and has hvaye for huve.
The long ─л in ├│с╣гadh─лm is expressly taught by Pr─Бt. iii. 6;
nagh─Бriс╣г─Бm is mentioned in the introduction to the fourth chapter
(add. note 4, at 11. 7).

Griffith

Here for sound health I invocate a living animating plant, Preserving, queller of disease, victorious, full of power and might.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Bless thou [him]; do not take hold; let him loose; even being
    thine, let him be one of completed years (?) here; O Bhava-and-┼Ыarva, be
    ye gracious; yield protection; driving away difficulty, bestow ye
    life-time.
Notes

The obscure -h─Бyas in b is translated here as if akin with
h─Бyana тМКcf. vs. 8 dтМЛ; the comm. glosses sarvah─Бyas with
sarvagati; the Petersburg Lexicons conjecture ‘having complete
liveliness or power.’ For s─Б╠Бn, the comm. reads sam тМКand joins it
with sс╣Ыj─Б╠Б, supplying pr─Бс╣З─БisтМЛ.

Griffith

Seize him not, but encourage and release him: here let him stay, though thine, in all his vigour. Bhava and Sarva, pity and protect him: give him full life and drive away misfortunes.

режрео рдЕрд╕реНрдореИ рдореГрддреНрдпреЛ ...{Loading}...
Whitney
Translation
  1. Bless thou this man, O death; pity him; let him go up from here;
    unharmed, whole-limbed, well-hearing, hundred-yeared by old age, let him
    attain enjoyment with himself.
Notes

Ppp. reads him for тАЩyam in b, and combines in d-e -h─Бyan─Б
тАЭtm-. The comm. paraphrases ─Бtm├бn─Б in d with anany─Бpekс╣гaс╕е san.
тМКRead as 8 + 11: 8 + 8 + 8.тМЛ

Griffith

Comfort him, Death, and pity him: let him arise and pass away, Unharmed, with all his members, hearing well, with old, may he through hundred years win profit with his soul.

Translation
  1. Let the missile of the gods avoid thee; I make thee pass from the
    welkin (r├бjas); I have made thee pass up out of death; removing afar
    the flesh-eating Agni, I set for thee an enclosure in order to living.

reads -haс╣Е j─лv- this time тМКcf. note to 1. 19. 4тМЛ because all his
saс╣Бhit─Б-mss. happen to agree in doing so; some of ours, however, do
not. The comm. explains r├бjasas in b as m┼лrch─Бlakс╣гaс╣З─Бd ─Бvaraс╣З─Бt.
тМКAt 97. 6, K─Бu┼Ы. gives in full, for use in case of a family quarrel (cf.
above, introd.), a verse whose first half agrees entirely with the
second half of this.тМЛ

Griffith

May the Gods’ missile pass thee by. I bring thee safe from the mist: from death have I preserved thee. Far have I banished flesh-consuming Agni: I place a rampart for thy life’s protection.

Whitney
Translation
  1. I make for thee breath-and-expiration, old age as [mode of] death,
    long life-time, welfare; all the messengers of Yama, sent forth by
    Vivasvant’s son, moving about, I drive away.
Notes

Ppp. reads in b jar─Бmс╣Ыtyum, and, in d, carat─Б тАЭr─Бn (i.e.
carata ─Бr─Бd?) apa.

Griffith

I give thee both the acts of breath, health, lengthened life, and death by age. All Yama’s messengers who roam around, sent by Vaivasvata, I chase away.

  1. Afar niggardliness, perdition, away seizure (gr─Б╠Бhi), the

d, eva for iva. Ppp. has tav─Бi тАЩva for tat tama iva. ‘Afar’
and ‘away’ in a, b anticipate as it were the ‘we smite away’ of
d.

Far off we drive Malignity, Destruction, Pisachas banqueters on flesh, and Grahi. And all the demon kind, the brood of sin, like darkness, we dispel.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Thy breath I win from immortal Agni, from long-lived J─Бtavedas, that
    thou mayest take no harm, mayest be immortal in alliance [with him]:
    that I make for thee; let that prove successful for thee.
Notes

A number of the mss. (including our Bp.E.D.) read с╣Ы╠Бс╣гy─Бs in c. Ppp.
has vanave for vanve in b, and yatr─Б at beginning of c.

Griffith

I win thy life from Agni, from the living everlasting Jatavedas. This I procure for thee, that thou, undying, mayst not suffer harm, that thou mayst be content, that all be well with thee.

резрек рд╢рд┐рд╡реЗ рддреЗ ...{Loading}...
Whitney
Translation
  1. Let heaven-and-earth be propitious to thee, not distressing,
    conferring fortune (? abhi┼Ыr─л╠Б); let the sun burn weal unto thee; let
    the wind blow weal to thy heart; let the heavenly waters, rich in
    fatness (p├бyas), flow propitious upon thee.
Notes

Ppp. combines s┼лry─Б тАЭtapatu in c, reads kс╣гaranti in e, and
adds further at the end ┼Ыiv─Бs te santv oс╣гadh─лс╕е. The comm. gives
adhi┼Ыriy─Бu in b, glossing it with pr─Бpta┼Ыr─лke ┼Ыr─лprade.

Griffith

Gracious to thee be Heaven and Earth, bringing no grief, and drawing nigh! Pleasantly shine the Sun for thee, the Wind blow sweetly to thy heart! Let the celestial Waters full of milk flow happily for thee.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Propitious to thee be the herbs; I have caught thee up from the
    lower unto the upper earth; there let both the ─Аdityas, sun and moon,
    defend thee.
Notes

Ppp. reads ─Б тАЩh─Бriс╣гam in b, and ati for abhi in c, and
combines -mas─Б ubh─Б at the end.

Griffith

Auspicious be the Plants to thee! I have upraised thee, borne thee from the lower to the upper earth: Let the two Sons of Aditi, the Sun and Moon, protect thee there.

    (n─лv├н) thou makest for thyself, that we make propitious unto thy body;

Griffith

Whatever robe to cover thee or zone thou makest for thyself, We make it pleasant to thy frame: may it be soft and smooth to touch.

can hardly be questioned, is the true one, although it is read by only

edition with them, ┼Ы├║mbham. SPP. accepts the unintelligible ┼Ы├║bham,

any sense. Ppp. further reads -┼Ыma┼Ыr┼л in b, and m─Бi тАЩnam for m─Б
nas in c. According to the distinct direction of the Pr─Бt. (ii.
76), we ought to read ─Б╠Бyuс╣г pr├б in d, and its authority is
sufficient to establish that as the true text, against both the
editions; half SPP’s authorities give it, though only one of ours (R.);
on such a point the mss. are often at odds, and their evidence of little
weight. The verse occurs also in several Gс╣Ыhya-S┼лtras, AGS. (i. 17. 16);
PCS. (ii. 1. 19), and HGS. (i. 9. 16); all read supe┼Ыas─Б in a; in

  1. What thou eatest (a┼Ы), what thou drinkest, of grain, milk of the
    plowingтАФwhat should be eaten, what should not be eatenтАФall food I make
    for thee poisonless.
Notes

The comm. reads strangely kс╣Ыchr─Бt instead of kс╣гsy─Бs in b.

Griffith

Thy food, thy drink, whate’er they be corn grown by cultivation, milk, Food eatable, uneatable, I make all poisonless for thee.

реиреж рдЕрд╣реНрдиреЗ рдЪ ...{Loading}...
Whitney
Translation
  1. Both to day and to night, to them both we commit thee. Defend ye
    this man for me from the ar─Б╠Бyas that seek to devour [him].
Notes

Some of SPP’s authorities, also the comm. and Ppp., read dadhmasi at
end of b. Ppp. further has r─Бyebhyas at beginning of c, and
nas (for me) in d. The comm. explains ar─Бyebhyas as =
adhanebhyo dhan─Бpahartс╣Ыbhyo v─Б.

Griffith

We give thee over as a charge to Day and Night, in trust to both. Keep him for me from stingy fiends, from those who fain would feed on him.

Whitney
Translation
  1. A hundred, a myriad years, two periods (yug├б), three, four, we
    make for thee; let Indra-and-Agni, let all the gods, approve thee, not
    showing enmity.
Notes

The second half-verse is i. 35. 4 c, d. The ‘periods’ here are not
at all likely to be those of the later chronology, though the comm.
naturally thinks them so. тМКAlternatively, he makes yug├й =
‘generations.’тМЛ Ppp. has santu for kс╣Ыс╣Зmas in b, and omits te
in c. The pada-mss. read t├й: ├бnu instead of te: ├бnu: compare
under i. 35. 4. тМКWe had a “sataс╕еpan╠Дkti” at vi. 20. 3.тМЛ

Griffith

A hundred, yea, ten thousand years we give thee, ages two, three, four. May Indra, Agni, all the Gods, with willing favour look on thee.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Unto autumn, unto winter, unto spring, unto summer, we commit thee;
    [be] the rains pleasant to thee, in which the herbs grow.
Notes

Ppp. has again dadhmasi in b.

Griffith

To Autumn we deliver thee, to Winter, Spring and Summer’s care. We trust thee with auspicious years wherein the plants and herbs grow up.

Whitney
Translation
  1. Let it protect thee from thy fellows, from witchcraft, from thy
    kinsmen; be thou undying, immortal, surviving; let not thy life-breaths
    (├бsu) leave thy body.
Notes

Ppp. reads sugantubhyas at end of b.

Griffith

Live very long, be healthy, be immortal: let not the vital breath forsake thy body.

Translation
  1. The deaths that are a hundred and one, the perditions (n─Бс╣гс╣нr─Б╠Б)
    that are to be over-passedтАФfrom that let the gods free thee, from Agni

Griffith

One and a hundred modes of death, dangers that may be over- come, May Gods deliver thee from this when Agni, dear to all men, bids.

Translation
  1. Agni’s body art thou, successful (p─Бrayiс╣гс╣З├║); demon-slayer art
    thou, rival-slayer, likewise expeller of disease, a remedy p┼лt├║dru by
    name.

vary between -с╣З├║ and -с╣З├║s at end of a; our edition reads -с╣З├║s

Griffith

Body of Agni prompt to save, slayer of fiends and foes art thou, Yea, banisher of malady, the healing balm called Putudru.
